Markets Begin Year With a Thud --- Concern about China's economy sparks global rout; Dow has worst start in eight years
Markets Begin Year With a Thud --- Concern about China's economy sparks global rout; Dow has
worst start in eight years
By E.S. Browning
1,082 words
5 January 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average began 2016 with its biggest opening-day loss since 2008, as financial
markets struggled with the same problems that disrupted them last year.
The Dow's 1.6% drop was triggered by a selloff in Asia and Europe following news that Chinese
manufacturing activity declined for a 10th consecutive month in December. China's currency, the yuan, pulled
back and the dollar rose against most major rivals.
Worries deepened that China's growth slowdown is harming the world economy and that its financial and
economic shakiness could roil markets further. China's growth rate is projected to fall to 6.3% in 2016,
according to the International Monetary Fund, from more than 10% in 2010. The Shanghai Composite Index
plummeted 6.9%, triggering circuit breakers that shut down the market. In early trading Tuesday in Asia,
stocks were up slightly after a drop at the open.
What concerns U.S. investors isn't just China. It is the spreading signs that weak global growth and a rising
U.S. dollar are harming U.S. corporate earnings and economic output, and could continue to do so this year.
On Monday, investors learned that U.S. manufacturing activity declined in December for the second
consecutive month and that construction spending fell in November.
"The near-term outlook for stocks is not so good. There are lots of drivers: the Federal Reserve tightening
monetary policy, China down, global instability, oil prices remaining weak and the stronger dollar," said
Krishna Memani, chief investment officer at OppenheimerFunds Inc., which oversees $222 billion.
"But the U.S. economy isn't falling apart," Mr. Memani said. He said he expects continued wage increases,
growth in earnings and economic output, and modest stock gains in 2016.
The declines were a reminder of China's strong pull on global markets months after a surprise devaluation of
its currency contributed to a period of severe turbulence last summer. Global stocks have rebounded from
those lows but remain vulnerable to concerns about the country's outlook.
After Monday's sharp stock selloff, China's central bank plans to inject 130 billion yuan in short-term funds to
help calm investors, according to people familiar with the matter.
Stock indexes took hits around the world Monday. Japan's Nikkei Stock Average fell 3.1%, Germany's DAX
index dropped 4.3% and the U.K.'s FTSE 100 index declined 2.4%.
The Dow tumbled 276.09 points to finish at 17148.94. It has fallen 3.2% over three consecutive trading days
for the worst three-session drop since the start of September.
Other U.S. indexes also declined Monday, as did oil and copper prices. The S&P 500 fell 1.5%. U.S.-traded
crude declined 0.8%, to $36.76 a barrel, after the weak manufacturing data out of China sparked concerns
that oil demand in the world's second-largest crude consumer could slow. Copper, which is sensitive to global
growth prospects, dropped 2.6%.
The 10-year U.S. Treasury note, a refuge in times of worry, rose 8/32 in price, pushing its yield down to
2.245%, as bond yields move inversely to prices. Gold, another haven asset, rose 1.4%, to $1,075.10 a troy
ounce.
"It is just unsettling on the first trading day of the year to get a bashing like we had today," said Andy Brooks,
head of U.S. stock trading at T. Rowe Price Group Inc., which oversees $725.5 billion. He put part of the
blame on the saber-rattling between Saudi Arabia and Iran. A rift between the two countries was sparked by
the Saudis' execution of a Shiite cleric.

--- Page 183 ---
Page 183 of 206 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Analysts are projecting a 0.6% decline in earnings for S&P 500 companies for 2015, the first yearly pullback
since the financial crisis, according to FactSet. Analysts forecast a rise of just 0.8% in 2016's first quarter and
4% in the second quarter. They expect stronger gains in the second half, but their forecasts have been
coming down. Forecasts were too optimistic early last year, and money managers fear they could be this
year, as well.
"I am worried about whether our earnings expectations are too high," said Leo Grohowski, chief investment
officer at BNY Mellon Wealth Management, which oversees $183 billion.
Earnings are suffering from the same double whammy that hurt them last year: oil and the dollar. Instead of
stabilizing near $50 a barrel, as many analysts had hoped, crude has continued to fall, down 27% in the past
12 months. Although cheaper oil is good for consumers, it damages profits in the stock market's energy
sector.
Meanwhile, with the Fed expected to raise interest rates three or even four times this year, the U.S. dollar
has been on the rise. Higher interest rates make dollars more attractive for global investors.
The stronger dollar is good for consumers because it makes imports cheaper. But it harms multinationals,
whose profits come partly in foreign currencies. As the dollar rises, foreign-currency profits are worth less in
dollar terms.
Expectations for higher interest rates alone have contributed to stock-market jitters. Higher rates make
borrowing more expensive for consumers and businesses. They also make it more expensive for investors to
buy stocks and bonds with borrowed money, which reduces the cash, or liquidity, available to markets.
Money managers have become particularly worried about liquidity in junk-bond and developing-country
markets.
Still, the start of January is typically a period of market strength, as institutions and retirement funds put new
money into stocks. Investors, typically an optimistic bunch, are usually setting themselves up for the new
year.
A problem that has stifled optimism in recent months, though, is that stocks have become expensive. The
S&P 500 finished 2015 at 23 times its component companies' net profits, far above the historical average of
15.5, according to Birinyi Associates. To push stock prices much higher, money managers said, they need
some kind of positive sign on corporate earnings, and right now the outlook is murky.
Mr. Grohowski of BNY Mellon said he expects U.S. stocks to rise this year, but he said, "Investors need to
brace themselves that 2016 could be another very volatile year."

Supermines Feed Vicious Metals Circle --- Giant sites, begun when prices were high, weigh down copper, iron ore
Supermines Feed Vicious Metals Circle --- Giant sites, begun when prices were high, weigh down
copper, iron ore
By John W. Miller
2,118 words
5 January 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CERRO VERDE, Peru -- In this volcanic desert, a dusty moonscape patrolled by bats, snakes and guanacos,
America's biggest miner is piling on to the new force in industrial resources: supermines. It's a strategy that
could be driving miners into the ground.
Freeport-McMoRan Inc. is completing a yearslong $4.6 billion expansion that will triple production at its Cerro
Verde copper mine, turning a once-tiny, unprofitable state mine into one of the world's top five copper
producers.
As Cerro Verde's towering concrete concentrators grind out copper to be made into pipes and wires in Asia, it
will add to production coming from newly built giant mines around the world, in a wave of supply that is
compounding the woes of the depressed mining sector.
Slowing growth in China and other emerging markets has dragged metals prices into a deep downturn, just a
few years after mining companies and their investors bet billions on a so-called supercycle, the seemingly
never-ending growth in demand for commodities.
Back then, miners awash in cheap money set out to build the biggest mines in history, extracting iron ore in
Australia, Brazil and West Africa, and copper from Chile, Peru, Indonesia, Arizona, Mongolia and the
Democratic Republic of Congo. They also expanded production of minerals such as zinc, nickel and bauxite,
which is mined to make aluminum.
Those giant mines are now giving the industry an extra-bad hangover during the bust. The big mines cost so
much to build and extract minerals so efficiently that mothballing them is unthinkable -- running them
generates cash to pay down debts, and huge mines are expensive to simply maintain while idle. But as a
result, their scale means they are helping miners dig themselves even deeper into the price trough by adding
to a glut.
The prolonged price slump has forced miners to make painful cuts. In December, Anglo American PLC, which
recently completed a supermine in Brazil that went over budget by $6 billion, announced 85,000 new job cuts,
asset sales and a suspended dividend.
On the same day, Rio Tinto PLC, which has built supermines in Western Australia, cut spending plans, while
in September, Glencore PLC suspended its dividend and raised $2.5 billion in stock as part of a plan to cut
debt.
Phoenix-based Freeport itself has felt the pressure. Executive Chairman and co-founder James R. Moffett
stepped down on Dec. 28, months after activist investor Carl Icahn took a large stake. The board approached
the wildcatter, who is known as "Jim Bob," about resigning, after nearly half a century with Freeport and its
predecessors.
When Freeport's Cerro Verde mine's production is fully ramped up this year, it will hit a billion pounds of
copper a year -- 3% of the world's production, even as copper prices sink to six-year lows.
The impact of mines like Cerro Verde is sweeping, including downward pressure on commodity prices until
the end of the decade, when supply is expected to slip as some older mines finally dry up. Companies' profit
margins are shrinking, many are writing down assets and more smaller players are likely to go belly-up,
according to ratings firms and analysts.
Investors who once bet on ever-increasing demand by backing miners have also been badly burned as share
prices have dropped and dividends have been gutted. The benchmark S&P/TSX Global Mining Index has
fallen 65% since 2010.

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 206 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Since 2007, mining companies have spent over $100 billion a year in capital expenditure, much of it to build
supermines, such as Rio Tinto and BHP Billiton PLC's expansions in the Pilbara region of Western Australia,
which require thousands of workers to fly in for stints in a remote desert, and Anglo American's Minas Rio in
Brazil, which involved a 325-mile iron ore slurry pipeline snaking its way though 32 municipalities.
Investment is still trickling in. Rio Tinto in December announced a $4.4 billion financing deal for its Oyu Tolgoi
copper and gold supermine in Mongolia.
"After nearly three decades of having to beg for capital, suddenly bankers and investors were throwing money
at miners and imploring them to invest in more and bigger projects," said Dick Evans, aformerboard member
of Rio Tinto, and now chairman of aluminum maker Constellium NV."The miners were like kidsin a candy
store."
At the same time, new mining technologies, like bigger haul trucks, shovels and other equipment, enabled the
building of facilities that could produce two or three times the copper or iron ore of the previous generation of
big mines.
Spread over 150,000 acres, Cerro Verde includes two pits that each could fitmultiple baseball stadiums; a
$500 million watertreatment plant half a mile long; and two hulking concrete and steel concentrators that look
like giant roller coasters and can extract copper from 360,000 tons of rock a day. The mine is capable of
consuming around 9% of all the electricity in Peru. A tour took six hours.
Cerro Verde is so big that "we're running into the limit of space and how many trucks you can put on ramps,"
said Red Conger, the chief operating officer responsible for the mine, on a recent mine visit.
Even before miners started building these giant mines, low interest rates helped spur a wave of mergers in
the industry, which had long been fragmented with thousands of small and midsize firms. Global players
including Rio Tinto, BHP, Glencore and Anglo American bulked up, and Freeport paid $25.9 billion for Phelps
Dodge in 2007. "We bought a company 2.5 times our size, and that was made possible only by cheap
financing," Freeport CEO Richard Adkerson said in an interview in November.
Freeport had only one mine, but it was massive: the Grasberg copper and gold mine in Indonesia, developed
by Mr. Moffett, a colorful Southerner known for his Elvis impersonations and his ability to forge political
connections in remote places.
Among Phelps Dodge's 11 copper mining properties was Cerro Verde, located amid dormant volcanoes 20
miles southwest of Arequipa, a picturesque Spanish colonial town dubbed the White City for the color of its
stone buildings.
Peru's mineral wealth has been known to foreign investors since the Spanish conquistadors invaded in the
16th century. Gold and silver were the big prizes until miners from the newly industrializing U.S. arrived early
last century with a new target: copper.
Discovered in the 19th century, the Cerro Verde deposit was considered so inconsequential it wasn't even
mined industrially until the 1970s. Then it became "a small mine that had been badly managed by the
Peruvian state," said Carlos Galvez, chief financial officer of Cia de Minas Buenaventura SAA, which has had
a minority stake in the mine since the 1990s. The site, which receives only 1.5 inches of rain a year, had
insufficient power and water.
Mr. Adkerson and his team, bullish copper advocates, believed Cerro Verde could be much bigger. "We
determined from the outset that Phelps Dodge had not been aggressive enough in drilling" to evaluate the
possibility of an expansion," said Mr. Adkerson. "And the benefit of the merger was that we could use the
[profits of the] Grasberg mine to support" Cerro Verde and other expansion projects.
Freeport's drillers concluded Cerro Verde had twice as much copper available as originally estimated, and in
2008, just over a year after the Phelps Dodge deal closed and with copper at $3.94 a pound, Freeport started
work on an expansion.
But then the financial crisis hit, pummeling demand for metals used in construction, and Freeport shelved the
project in March 2009. The price of copper had fallen to $1.40 a pound, below the expected cost of extraction,
guaranteeing that a bigger Cerro Verde would lose money.
Copper prices began to rise as Asian economies led a global recovery in 2009, and demand from China
soared for iron ore and copper, the two minerals needed in huge quantities for industrialization. By March
2010, Freeport rebooted the Cerro Verde project. The company also undertook expansion projects elsewhere
in Peru, and in Arizona and Congo.

--- Page 186 ---
Page 186 of 206 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It took three years for Freeport to secure permits and to develop an engineering plan that harnessed the new
technologies and secured the water needed to mine on such a massive scale.
Mining companies consider Peru one of the most difficult countries to navigate as a foreign investor. The
country has a long history of anti-mining activism, dating back to fighting the pillaging Spaniards in the 16th
century.
To obtain permits, Freeport built a $500 million wastewater treatment plant that serves the mine. Separately,
it also designed and paid for a $125 million plant that cleans water from a local river for Arequipa.
Majura Chua, 43, runs a small shop selling toilet paper, candy and soft drinks down the road from one of the
new water plants. This October, she was hooked up to a pipe. "We can take showers now," she said. Before
that, she had to pour buckets into a reservoir on top of the house.
The company also lobbied local residents and key officials, including the local archbishop. People in one
shantytown recall that in 2010Freeportsent them Christmas presents: soccer balls for boys and Barbies for
girls.
Freeport said it has a tradition of sending Christmas gifts to the children of the area, and that it also sponsors
educational programs.
Construction didn't start until March 2013. But copper was already in another downturn, falling from a peak of
$4.48 a pound in February of 2011 as China slowed and new supply came online.
The scale and complexity of the project slowed the work. The area, still scattered with volcanic ash from
eruptions centuries old, was so mountainous, "we needed 10 and a half months just to create the flat space to
build," said Mr. Conger, the chief operating officer. Cerro Verde is in an earthquake zone, and everything had
to be designed to withstand a 9.5 Richter quake.
A project of this enormousness wouldn't have been possible years ago, and might not have been cost
effective, even at healthy metals prices, according to Mr. Evans, the former Rio Tinto board member. "Now
there are bigger trucks, faster drills and computer controlled processing equipment which can support larger
scale mines," he said.
The mine's huge scale keeps its operating costs down, at under $1.50 per pound. That means evenwith
copper prices now just above $2 a pound, a six-year low, it will continue to make money on an operational
basis.
But the production of giant mines will help keep metals prices low "because it will help to keep output high,"
said analyst Dan Rohr of Morningstar Inc.
After 15 consecutive profitable quarters, Freeport has lost money in the past three. The company is also
loaded down with $20.7 billion in debt, much of it accumulated during its 2013 acquisitions of energy
companies McMoRan Exploration Co. and Plains Exploration & Production Co. in deals valued at $19 billion.
In 2014, to help pay for the Cerro Verde expansion, Cerro Verde obtained a five-year $1.8 billion loan.
Mr. Adkerson said the company "financed Cerro Verde principally with cash flows" in addition to that line of
credit. Freeport's "copper expansions are not driven by debt," he said.
But Freeport's quarterly losses and debt load have put pressure on management. In August, the company
said it would slash its 2016 capital spending by 29%, cut about 10% of its U.S. workforce -- more than 1,500
jobs -- and lower production.
The day Freeport made that announcement, Mr. Icahn, the activist investor, let Mr. Adkerson know he had
bought an 8.5% stake in the company, worth roughly $900 million at the time, making him one of the
company's biggest shareholders.
In October, Freeport said it would cut the size of its board and focus on its copper business, and explore all
options for its energy business, including a spinoff or sale of a stake.
"We're in a tough marketplace, we have too much debt, we have to come up with business strategies about
how to manage our balance sheet in a difficult environment," said Mr. Adkerson.
But he added he believed the supply of copper would dwindle in the future. "You need large-scale mining and
large-scale processing to be competitive," he said. "And that's what we have in Cerro Verde."

Another Bad Year for Gold
Another Bad Year for Gold
By Alistair MacDonald
537 words
4 January 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- The price of gold traded higher on the last day of a year in which the precious metal fell to its
third consecutive annual loss, declining on the back of the new U.S. interest-rate cycle that will likely keep the
pressure up in 2016.
Gold for February delivery closed up less than 0.1% at $1,060.20 a troy ounce in New York last week amid
thin volumes as many investors remained idle during the Christmas and New Year holidays.
Gold ended the year down around 10%, which pushed the metal to a level not seen since February 2010.
A key factor behind that fall was anticipation that the U.S. would see its first rate rise in nine years.
Gold offers no income, making it less competitive with yield-bearing securities like Treasurys. This
anticipation also helped push up the dollar, which has gained nearly 25% in the past 18 months., making
greenback-denominated investments more expensive for many investors.
With the U.S. just at the beginning of a new rate-rising cycle that pressure is likely to keep up on gold into
this year, analysts say.
"Overall the headwinds for gold remain for next year, given interest rates and the dollar," said Xiao Fu, head
of commodity markets strategy at BOCI Global Commodities (UK) Ltd.
Gold's ability to break out of its trough will depend to a large degree on how aggressive the Federal Reserve
is with its rate rises.
Currently, futures prices indicate investors expect two or three rate rises by the end of 2016, taking short-term
interest rates up to around 1%, a relatively sluggish rate of increase.
For analysts who expect a relatively more gentle path from the Fed, the metal has the potential to end this
year on a more positive note than 2015.
"The concerns around rates may have been overdone," said William Tankard, a precious- metals analyst at
GFMS Commodities Research & Forecasts. Mr. Tankard sees the gold price averaging around $1,200 an
ounce this year.
When weighing rate moves, central bankers in most of the developed world have been concerned about the
lack of inflation in their economies. Most analysts don't expect a significant pickup in prices, depriving gold
of a factor that has consistently boosted its price in recent decades.
Still, compared with other major commodities, gold has actually held up.
Copper ended the year around 25% lower, while iron ore fell around 43% and Brent oil, the global crude
benchmark, closed down around 36%.
Among other precious metals, platinum was down by more than 26% and silver was down nearly 13% last
year.
Gold has held up because it is mainly used as a store of value and has few industrial uses, thus leaving it
less exposed to concerns over the Chinese economy and oversupply factors that have buffeted other
resources.
Among the other precious metals last week, March silver settled down 0.3% at $13.80 a troy ounce and April
platinum rose 2.4% to $893.20 a troy ounce, while March palladium was up 2.4% at $562 a troy ounce.

Venezuela Currency Bust Fuels Plastic Surgery Boom Boom --- Stringent economic controls help create hub for low-cost nips and tucks
Venezuela Currency Bust Fuels Plastic Surgery Boom Boom --- Stringent economic controls help
create hub for low-cost nips and tucks
By Anatoly Kurmanaev
1,132 words
31 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
PUERTO ORDAZ, Venezuela -- This country's industrial capital was once known for steel and aluminum.
Today, people come here for the silicone.
Stringent currency controls, which have slashed trade and closed plants in this city of 700,000, have created
an unexpected new boom sector: plastic surgery. A shortage of dollars allows a foreigner to sell greenbacks
on the black market for 130 times more local bolivars than the official rate, giving them a Miss Venezuela
beauty treatment on a Cinderella budget.
"I would come back 10 times over for such results," said Brazilian canteen owner Sidileia Da Silva, 27 years
old, after undergoing three plastic surgeries on the same day for $1,800. "Everything went great."
Hundreds of women a month like Ms. Da Silva brave a 500-mile journey through 16 military checkpoints and
illegal gold-mining hamlets of Venezuela's lawless southern savanna to this riverside city to seek perfection
on the cheap. A standard package of breast implants, liposuction and the buttocks injections known as a
"Boom Boom" is a third of the cost in Brazil, said Dr. Oscar Hurtado, who runs a plastic surgery clinic here.
"Most of our clients are poor Brazilians who could never afford this at home," said Dr. Hurtado, 50, who
speaks Portuguese and employs a bilingual staff. "Here they realize their dreams."
As the first real city north of the Brazilian border, Puerto Ordaz is a natural hub. To gin up some
much-needed cash, doctors' practices offer the Brazilians transport, accommodation, nurses, hairdressers,
shopping therapy and even trips to Caribbean beaches.
The hard currency the Brazilians bring helps the locals survive the deepest recession since independence in
1810 and 200% inflation, by far the world's highest.
Engineer Maria Contreras said she complements her $40 monthly salary at the state steel plant by nursing
post-op Brazilians at home for $18 a day. She said she is also learning Portuguese, adding, "We try to create
a family atmosphere."
System maintenance manager Edgardo Ibarra said he triples his wage at the aluminum smelter by driving the
women on his days off back and forth to the border, as they hide their dollars and Brazilian reais inside bras
to pass regular army searches. The soldiers, mostly looking for outlaws with contraband and weapons, go
easy on the women, who aren't breaking any laws by crossing borders for treatment.
"It used to be a dream to land a job in the heavy industries here," said Mr. Ibarra, 51. "Now, we have to look
for alternatives to survive."
Female appearance is an obsession in this nation of soap operas and beauty queens. Venezuela has the
most Miss World winners in the history of the pageant, with the national beauty competition the most-watched
TV program. Special beauty academies cater to girls as young as 6, and breast implants are a typical
teenage birthday present.
For the Brazilians, the biggest seller among the Venezuelan beauty treatments is the "Boom Boom" --
injections of a woman's own fat into her buttocks to make them bigger. The rear view is essential in Brazil,
which holds a national competition called Miss BumBum.
Venezuela's rock-bottom wages and real-estate costs make plastic surgeries profitable when paid in foreign
currency, even after importing all the inputs at the black-market rate, doctors said.
"I feel like a rock star here," said Olika Davila, a Puerto Ordaz plastic surgeon born into a family of
steelworkers.

--- Page 191 ---
Page 191 of 206 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Nine out of 10 of her patients are Brazilians, up from almost none three years ago. Foreign clients allowed
her to more than double her staff to seven since then, while Venezuela's economy lost a quarter of its gross
domestic product under the policies of President Nicolas Maduro, the handpicked successor to the late
socialist President Hugo Chavez.
The flood of Brazilian women is also prompting doctors like gynecologist Julio Palao to open cosmetic
practices on the side.
Nose surgeon Jose Manuel Ferreira is building a new general health clinic amid abandoned residential
construction sites downtown and near where hundreds of people line up for scarce food outside a market.
The investment is only viable if the treatment for the locals is subsidized by aesthetic procedures for
higher-paying Brazilians, he said. Dr. Ferreira said his practice has benefited from Brazilians who come for
thinner, "European" looking noses.
The Ferrominera Encampment on the edge of town is home to 127 guesthouses, with four in five beds
occupied by recovering Brazilian women, said Candida Angel, the head of the state tourism association. The
patients hire their own nurses to attend to them.
Many also hire watchmen. In a country with one of the world's highest crime rates, foreigners are prime
targets of armed robberies, sometimes even inside the hospitals.
In Puerto Ordaz's ChileMex clinic, custodian Leila Do Santos kept an eye on her Brazilian client as she
waited in the hallway to see a plastic surgeon. "I tell them not to open their mouths, not to speak Portuguese,
carry jewelry or cellphones," said Ms. Do Santos, a fixer and chaperone who organizes everything for the
surgery and brings her clients in and out of the country. "Here, Brazilians mean money."
Most Brazilians who come here for surgery are from Brazil's rural Roraima state on the border. Many are
housekeepers, kitchen maids and shop assistants. "Many of these women live in houses with earthen floors
and corrugated iron roofs," said Dr. Davila. "This is the only city in the world where plastic surgery is not a
luxury" for them.
The clientele began to change this year as word of Venezuelan surgeons' skill and price spread further down
the continent. More middle-class women are arriving from Manaus, a metropolis of two million people further
south in the Amazon jungle, doctors said.
"The housewives of Manaus sense competition when they see their housekeepers all pampered up," said Dr.
Ferreira. "They start asking where did they get those curves, and end up coming here."
The boom has been fostered by social media. Each doctor's practice manages at least one WhatsApp group
of around 300 current and prospective clients, allowing them to ask questions, send photos of their bodies, fix
prices and arrange transport.
"I want a butt just like that!!!" wrote a woman called Alessandra from Roraima in Dr. Hurtado's chat group, in
response to a picture of her recently operated-on friend. Others responded with copious emojis of red hearts,
smiley faces and clapping hands.
"This is going to be a great year," Dr. Hurtado said, "thanks to our Brazilian clients."

The Intelligent Investor: The Simple, Best Way To Predict The Market
The Intelligent Investor: The Simple, Best Way To Predict The Market
Jason Zweig
877 words
26 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
Yield to maturity is the implied interest rate that makes the present value of all of a bond's future cash flows
equal to the bond's current market price. The Dec. 26 Intelligent Investor column incorrectly defined yield to
maturity as interest income divided by market price.
(WSJ Jan. 5, 2016)
(END)
'Tis the season to make guesses.
You might think that to predict stock or bond returns like a Wall Street strategist at year end, you need to
mutter obscure incantations over a computer spewing out long equations bristling with Greek letters. But what
if simplicity beats complexity?
Twenty-five years ago, John C. Bogle, founder of Vanguard Group, the giant investment firm, began
publishing studies of stock and bond performance, seeking to determine the "sources of return" that have
driven their results.
Mr. Bogle updates his findings in an article in the latest issue of the Journal of Portfolio Management.
Combing through returns all the way back to 1915, he found that you can explain the past returns on stocks --
and predict their future returns with decent accuracy -- by taking only three factors into account.
At first, that sounds so childishly simple it has to be wrong. But researchers in many fields have found that
extremely basic formulas are often superior at making predictions about complicated systems.
The first of Mr. Bogle's three elements is the starting yield, or annual dividends divided by stock price,
currently about 2.2%. Second is earnings growth, which historically has averaged about 4.7%. Together
those sources constitute what he calls the "investment return," because they are based on the cash that
companies generate.
Third is the "speculative return," or any change in the mob psychology of how much investors want to pay for
stocks. The S&P 500 is priced nowadays at about $23 per $1 of earnings per share, or a price/earnings ratio
of nearly 23.
If that ratio rose to 25 over the coming decade, that would be roughly a 10% increase -- boosting stock
returns by about one percentage point annually. On the other hand, if the P/E fell to 20, that decline of more
than 10% from today's level would lower the next decade's returns by about one percentage point per year.
That 2.2% dividend yield, plus the 4.7% earnings-growth rate, equals a smidgen under 7%. If market
valuations rise one percentage point annually, that would take average returns up to about 8%; if they fall the
same amount, total returns would drop to about 6%.
As for bonds, Mr. Bogle has found that essentially all you need to know is their yield to maturity, or interest
income divided by market price. Analyzing 10-year periods back to 1906, he found that at least 90% of the
subsequent return on bonds could be explained by their initial yields; capital gains and losses barely
registered. So, with the Barclays U.S. Aggregate index of government and corporate bonds yielding 2.6%,
that's the baseline expectation.
None of these figures count inflation, which the Federal Reserve has targeted at 2% annually. Subtract that
to account for loss of purchasing power, and stocks look likely to return an average of about 5% annually;
bonds, less than 1%.

--- Page 196 ---
Page 196 of 206 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Note that these are expectations for the coming decade, not the next year -- which is hard to predict.
By so clearly decomposing expected return into the factors that drive it, Mr. Bogle provides a model anyone
can adapt. "If you don't like my numbers [other than dividend yield]," he says, "you can put in your own."
Do you think technology will enable future earnings to grow faster than in the past, or that investors might be
willing to pay more for those earnings? Do you instead believe the future will be worse? Then ratchet the
earnings-growth rate and P/E contribution accordingly.
You can apply the same principles to any financial asset. Take commodities. Historically, a diversified basket
of such assets as oil, gold and soybeans has had four main sources of return, says Ryan Larson of Research
Affiliates, a firm in Newport Beach, Calif., whose strategies are used to manage about $154 billion.
One comes from renewing commodity contracts over time -- the "roll return." Another is the interest earned on
collateral to finance the purchase of commodities. Third, market prices go up or down. Finally, a commodities
portfolio manager can sell some of whatever has gone up the most and use the proceeds to buy some of
whatever has gone down the most -- a technique called rebalancing.
The roll return on many commodities has gone negative, while the interest on collateral is nil. Research
Affiliates expects commodities to return 3.6% annually over the next decade, after inflation. But nearly all of
that assumption, as Mr. Larson concedes, depends on rebalancing and changes in market price; it gets no
tailwind from the investment factors of roll or collateral return. As of now, you will need mob psychology to
make good money in commodities.
As 2016 approaches, ignore Wall Street's elaborate guesswork. Sharpen your own pencil, think long-term
and keep it simple.
Document J000000020151226ebcq0001y

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 206 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

A rally in oil prices fired up energy stocks.

The day stood out against a brutal month, and year, for oil prices and energy stocks, thanks largely to a
global glut of crude oil. For the month, oil prices have slumped 10% and energy stocks have fallen 7.1%.
Wednesday's rally helped drive another good day for the broader market.
The Dow rose 185.34 points, or 1.1%, to 17602.61. The S&P 500 gained 25.32 points, or 1.2%, to 2064.29.
The Nasdaq Composite advanced 44.82 points, or 0.9%, to 5045.93. All three indexes are on track for their
biggest weekly percentage gains since the week ended Nov. 20.
Wednesday's gains came on the last full trading day before the Christmas holiday. The U.S. stock market will
close early on Thursday and remain closed Friday for Christmas Day.
Within energy, beleaguered exploration-and-production companies were the biggest gainers.
Of 80 U.S.-listed oil-and-gas producers, all but two -- a company in bankruptcy that lost 22% and another
smaller producer that was unchanged -- rose on the day. More than half of those 80 companies gained more
than 10%.
Some of the day's biggest gainers are among the year's biggest losers as well as the most heavily shorted
stocks in the sector, suggesting that some of their climb could have come from investors buying shares to
close out bets that they would fall.
Chesapeake Energy, for instance, is down 78% on the year and had more than a third of its shares sold short
as of Tuesday, according to S&P Capital IQ. It rose 11%, or 42 cents, to $4.40 Wednesday.
The rise to a two-week high for U.S. oil prices was prompted in part by a U.S. Energy Information
Administration report that said the nation's crude stockpiles shrank by 5.9 million barrels last week, surprising
investors who expected a slight buildup to inventories.
It was unclear, though, whether the smaller stockpile represents a slowdown in production that would buoy
energy prices in the longer term or a year-end anomaly that has more to do with Gulf Coast refiners preparing
for tax season.
Like retailers that let their inventories dwindle at year-end to lower their tax bills, a million-barrel-a-day decline
in imports last week suggests Gulf Coast refiners are reducing their stockpiles of crude, said Tim Evans,
analyst at Citi Futures Perspective in New York.
"This looks very much like the typical end-of-year storage play, with Gulf Coast refiners slashing inventories
to mitigate state ad valorem taxes on year-end crude stocks," he wrote in a note to clients.
This December has been turbulent for financial markets. Oil prices tumbled to multiyear lows, and a rout in
the junk-bond market intensified earlier this month, before easing.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average has moved by at least 100 points for nine consecutive sessions. The
blue-chip index's biggest move in that period came two days after the Federal Reserve decided to raise
interest rates for the first time in nearly a decade.

Gold Won't Take Gold in Metals Race
Heard on the Street
Gold Won't Take Gold in Metals Race
By Spencer Jakab
290 words
21 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Which metal has the most mettle? While they are all looking awfully brittle these days, the surprising answer
is gold. In the past year and a half, the traditional store of value has lost a mere 16% -- a smaller decline than
seven other widely traded metals. Iron ore, for instance, is down 57%.
The reason that is odd is gold's appeal, or lack thereof, in the current financial environment. When interest
rates were zero, the opportunity cost of owning gold was low since returns on cash were minuscule. As an
added bonus for those who feared loose monetary policy would spur price rises, gold's traditional role as a
bulwark against runaway inflation won it fans.
But with the Fed having signaled last week's rate rise for some time, gold should have suffered more than
other metals. Why hasn't it? Gold's uselessness has been its saving grace.
Copper, iron ore, nickel and zinc are suffering from weak end demand and a big buildup in supply. Even
fellow precious metals silver and platinum are weaker than gold because both have alternate industrial uses.
The silver lining for silver and other industrial metals is that the demand slump is temporary. And investment
cutbacks today contain seeds of a future bull market.
By contrast, nearly all the gold mined since antiquity is above ground in some form. Inflation may rear its
head some day and gold may be a haven, but that would boost the dollar price of industrial metals, too.
Gold has held up pretty well. But it may soon lose its luster as the most modest loser in a metals bear
market.

Commodities Drop Anew --- Raw-materials prices fall broadly as Fed tightening stokes worries about demand
Commodities Drop Anew --- Raw-materials prices fall broadly as Fed tightening stokes worries about
demand
By Ira Iosebashvili
260 words
18 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Prices for most raw materials fell, a day after the decision by the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates
compounded the pressure on an asset class already suffering from weak demand and persistent supply gluts.
Thursday's declines kick off the final two weeks of a year that has been marked by sharp drops in commodity
prices, driven in part by a deepening slowdown in China, the world's largest consumer of most raw materials.
Investors fear that the Fed's tighter monetary policy will further sap demand from emerging markets and
many raw materials, as it boosts the dollar and makes it more difficult for countries with debt denominated in
the U.S. currency to meet obligations. At the same time, the stronger greenback makes it more profitable for
foreign producers to pump out commodities, many of which are priced in dollars, further adding to market
supply and likely pushing down prices.
Light, sweet crude for January delivery fell 57 cents, or 1.6%, to $34.95 a barrel on the New York Mercantile
Exchange, the lowest settlement since February 2009. The declines came after stockpile data showed no
pause in the flood of excess oil.
Gold settled at a six-year low, down 2.5%, to $1,050.80 a troy ounce, weighed down by the prospect of
higher interest rates and scant inflation. Other metals, such as copper and nickel, also dropped. Cocoa,
coffee and cotton all notched losses.

Fed Rally Evaporates as Stocks Drop
Thursday's Markets
Fed Rally Evaporates as Stocks Drop
By Leslie Josephs and Chelsey Dulaney
512 words
18 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks fell after a three-day winning streak, hit by another decline in energy shares.
The retreat came a day after investors greeted the Federal Reserve's decision to raise interest rates for the
first time in almost a decade with a broad rally. Thursday's losses more than erased the previous session's
gains.
"I think yesterday there was a bit of euphoria" with the Fed raising rates after so much anticipation, said Mike
O'Rourke, chief market strategist at JonesTrading. "We're returning back to the patterns that had existed
beforehand," he said.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average declined 253.25 points, or 1.4%, to 17495.84. The S&P 500 fell 31.18
points, or 1.5%, to 2041.89, pushing the index back into negative territory for the year. The Nasdaq
Composite slipped 68.58 points, or 1.4%, to 5002.55.
Portfolio managers were selling companies in the beaten-down energy sector as they raced to limit investors'
losses ahead of the two holiday-shortened weeks left in 2015, said Gordon Charlop, managing director at
Rosenblatt Securities.
"It's one thing to say 'I was wrong,' but it's another thing to tell them 'I got it wrong and I'm still carrying those
names,'" said Mr. Charlop.
Energy stocks led the S&P 500 lower with a 2.5% decline as U.S. oil prices slipped 1.6% to $34.95 a barrel.
Energy shares have fallen nearly 11% so far this month as the commodities rout deepened.
Caterpillar, which makes construction and mining equipment, led the Dow lower, shedding $2.82, or 4.2%, to
$64.90. Chevron lost 2.90, or 3.1% to 90.54. Mining company Freeport McMoRan posted the biggest decline
in the S&P 500, losing 57 cents, or 8.5% to 6.12.
The Stoxx Europe 600 rose 1.2%.
In Asia early Friday, Japan's Nikkei 225 Stock Average was down 0.5%, while Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index
and South Korea's Kospi were down 0.4%. The Shanghai Composite was up 0.3%.
While ultralow interest rates have boosted equity markets in recent years, investors were reassured by the
Fed's confidence in the world's biggest economy and its plans to raise rates gradually.
Some investors remained worried that the Fed was ending a period of near-zero rates at a time of tepid
global growth and troubles in emerging markets and junk bonds.
"Maybe it is the first step in a long journey to equilibrium," said Riad Younes, portfolio manager at RSQ
International Equity Fund. "But the question is: Is it a premature step? We're still living in challenging times."
In currency markets, the euro fell 0.6% against the dollar to $1.0821, while the dollar rose against the yen.
Gold prices fell 2.5% to $1050.80 an ounce. Tighter monetary policy makes gold less attractive to investors
as it competes with yield-bearing assets.

MoneyBeat: Rate Rise Winners and Losers
MoneyBeat: Rate Rise Winners and Losers
563 words
17 December 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Federal Reserve may have just knocked over the first domino.
The Fed's decision to raise rates on Wednesday will have broad repercussions for stocks and bonds, on Main
Street and Wall Street, and for economies in all corners of the world.
For some, the impact could be felt right away, and for others, the shakeout may be felt more acutely over
time, especially if the Fed follows the first rate rise with sustained tightening.
Here is a roundup from Wall Street Journal staffers about who wins and who loses when rates increase:
-- Banks. Lenders have been yearning for the Federal Reserve to start raising rates for years to stem the
decline in their net interest margin, an important measure of banks' profitability. The interest rates banks
charge on many loans are directly tied to the Fed's target rate, meaning they immediately earn more interest
on those items, while deposit rates move more gradually. Goldman Sachs Group Inc. analysts recommends
owning the stocks of banks whose depositors are likely to stick around: Bank of America Corp., J.P. Morgan
Chase & Co., Wells Fargo & Co., PNC Financial Services Group, Inc. and E*Trade Financial Corp.
-- Peter Rudegeair
-- Gold. Prices slumped to five-year lows in recent weeks as expectations of higher rates from the Fed hit
fever-pitch. Bets that benefit if gold prices fall outpaced wagers that gold will rally by a record margin of
17,949 contracts on Dec. 1, according to CFTC data. Tighter monetary policy is likely to make it harder for
gold, which doesn't pay interest, to compete with yield-bearing assets like Treasury bonds.
-- Tatyana Shumsky
-- Housing market. Higher short-term interest rates won't wreck housing, and in the near-term might have
hardly any impact at all. For one, the rate of mortgages, as with those of other long-term loans, have only an
indirect relationship with short-term rates and are influenced more heavily by factors such as inflation
expectations and economic growth. The rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage -- which last week was at
3.95% according to Freddie Mac -- almost surely already is pricing in expectations of a Fed move on
Wednesday. That said, over the next year, some economists expect mortgage rates to rise about half a
percentage point. That would hurt home prices as borrowers would need to buy a cheaper house to keep the
same monthly payment.
-- Joe Light
-- Life insurers. Few companies are rooting for a sustained rise in interest rates as loudly as U.S. insurers,
and life insurers are leading the cheers. Most insurers earn substantial income from investing premiums, and
they typically favor high-quality bonds, whose yields have plummeted in recent years amid the sustained low
interest-rate environment. Life insurers depend more heavily on investment income than do car, home and
some sorts of business insurers. That is because life insurers can collect premiums for decades before
paying out a claim, and rely on that investment income to make a policy profitable. For many older policies on
life insurers' books, the companies expected to earn far-higher yields than newly invested cash fetches today.
Low rates also raise the cost of running risk-management programs. Such hedging programs are important
for those life insurers selling certain types of income-stream guarantees.
-- Leslie Scism
